This is safe because the bundle contains zero operator-supplied secrets: build.rs bakes only the git SHA and build timestamp (both public per Section 12.3), and the auth secret / public-host / LA host / cert SANs live in runtime state that the bundle never sees. The defense surface is the API.
  • Authenticated HTTP routes (/v1/info, /v1/capabilities): the token is presented exclusively via the Authorization: Bearer <secret> request header. A token in the query string is rejected, even if the secret matches, with 400 Bad Request and body "auth: token must be in Authorization header for HTTP routes; query parameter rejected".
  • WebSocket-upgrade route (/v1/signal): the token is presented exclusively via the ?token=<secret> query-string parameter (because the browser WebSocket Application Programming Interface forbids setting request headers on the upgrade). A token in the Authorization header for this route is rejected with the symmetric error "auth: token must be in ?token= query parameter for WebSocket upgrade; Authorization header rejected".

Auth middleware skips both /v1/health and the static SPA bundle entirely (mounted on no_auth_routes, which is merged BEFORE the auth-layered router; see the code block below).

Implementation: the axum router is built with the no-auth routes mounted FIRST, then a .merge(...) with the auth-requiring routes that have from_fn_with_state(state, auth_layer) applied:

use tower_http::set_header::SetResponseHeaderLayer;
use tower_http::trace::TraceLayer;
use axum::http::{header, HeaderValue};

// The static SPA bundle (index.html, the fingerprinted JS / WASM / CSS) is
// served WITHOUT authentication. Reason: a browser's first GET to
// `https://host:8443/#token` carries no Authorization header (the fragment
// is client-side per RFC 3986; the WASM client is what would set the header,
// and it hasn't loaded yet). If the bundle were behind auth, the very first
// page-load would 401 and the demo would be unreachable. The secret is the
// URL fragment token, not the bundle's bytes; the bundle contains no
// operator-supplied strings (build.rs bakes only the git SHA + build time,
// both public), no SAN list, no LA host, no auth secret. The auth-gated
// surface is the API: /v1/info (cert SANs, deployment detail), /v1/capabilities
// (prompt templates), and /v1/signal (the WebSocket).
// axum 0.8 panics on `nest_service("/", ...)` — root-nesting is intentionally
// disabled. The replacement is `fallback_service`, which catches any path
// that doesn't match an explicit route (i.e., everything under `/` that
// isn't `/v1/health` here). ServeDir satisfies the required
// `Service<Request, Error = Infallible>` bound.
let no_auth_routes = Router::new()
    .route("/v1/health", get(health_handler))
    .fallback_service(ServeDir::new(dist_dir));

let authed_routes = Router::new()
    .route("/v1/info",         get(info_handler))
    .route("/v1/capabilities", get(capabilities_handler))
    .route("/v1/signal",       get(signal_ws_handler))
    .layer(axum::middleware::from_fn_with_state(state.clone(), auth_layer));

// In tower/axum, `.layer()` calls compose innermost-last but read outermost-first.
// The order below is OUTERMOST-FIRST in source order: SetResponseHeaderLayer for
// every security header (CSP, X-Frame-Options, X-Content-Type-Options, etc.),
// then TraceLayer, then the merged routes (which themselves carry the auth_layer
// innermost). Chain order: security headers (outermost) → trace → auth (innermost).
let app = no_auth_routes
    .merge(authed_routes)
    .layer(SetResponseHeaderLayer::overriding(
        header::CONTENT_SECURITY_POLICY,
        HeaderValue::from_str(csp::CSP_HEADER.as_str())
            .context("CSP header value")?,
    ))
    .layer(SetResponseHeaderLayer::overriding(
        header::X_FRAME_OPTIONS,
        HeaderValue::from_static("DENY"),
    ))
    .layer(SetResponseHeaderLayer::overriding(
        header::X_CONTENT_TYPE_OPTIONS,
        HeaderValue::from_static("nosniff"),
    ))
    .layer(SetResponseHeaderLayer::overriding(
        header::REFERRER_POLICY,
        HeaderValue::from_static("no-referrer"),
    ))
    .layer(SetResponseHeaderLayer::overriding(
        header::HeaderName::from_static("permissions-policy"),
        HeaderValue::from_static(/* the canonical Permissions-Policy literal: camera=(self), microphone=(), ... */ "..."),
    ))
    // Strip the Server header (defense-in-depth; reduces fingerprinting).
    .layer(SetResponseHeaderLayer::overriding(
        header::SERVER,
        HeaderValue::from_static(""),
    ))
    .layer(
        // Record the request PATH only, never req.uri() — the latter Displays the
        // ?token= query (DefaultMakeSpan does exactly `uri = %request.uri()`
        // at DEBUG level, tower-http 0.6.11 src/trace/make_span.rs:98), which would
        // put the shared secret into `docker logs` the moment RUST_LOG is raised to
        // debug/trace. Path-only closes that footgun everywhere (§3 secret-hygiene).
        // (The Authorization: Bearer header is already never logged — headers are
        // off by default in DefaultMakeSpan — so the query string is the only leak
        // surface.)
        TraceLayer::new_for_http().make_span_with(
            |req: &axum::http::Request<axum::body::Body>| tracing::info_span!(
                "request",
                method  = %req.method(),
                path    = %req.uri().path(),   // path() strips the ?query
                version = ?req.version(),
            )
        )
    )
    .with_state(state);

The csp::CSP_HEADER reference is a OnceLock<String> built at process startup (Section 16.5) that includes the build.rs-injected CSP_SCRIPT_HASHES. The Permissions-Policy literal is the verbatim Section 16.3 string. Cache-Control headers are NOT applied via SetResponseHeaderLayer because they vary by content type (Section 16.4); they are set per-handler instead (tower_http::set_header::SetResponseHeaderLayer::if_not_present + per-route layers, OR direct HeaderMap manipulation in each handler — pick one consistently at implementation time).

This eliminates the "two ways to do the same thing" pattern AND honors the operational requirement that HEALTHCHECK and external monitors reach /v1/health without credentials. 
pub async fn auth_layer(
    axum::extract::State(state):     axum::extract::State<Arc<AppState>>,
    axum::extract::ConnectInfo(peer):axum::extract::ConnectInfo<std::net::SocketAddr>,
    req:                             axum::http::Request<axum::body::Body>,
    next:                            axum::middleware::Next,
) -> Result<axum::response::Response, (axum::http::StatusCode, String)> {
    let ip = peer.ip();

    // The per-IP rate limiter is consulted ONLY on a FAILED auth (below), never
    // on a successful or no-credential request. Gating only FAILURES is what lets
    // an arbitrary number of correct-token viewers behind ONE NAT/CGNAT egress IP
    // all attach without throttling — the §2 "everybody else visiting … will also
    // have the video stream" requirement. (The constant-time compare at §17.2
    // already neutralizes the only timing leak the former check-before-compare
    // guarded against; the pass/fail bit is intentionally public via the
    // 200-vs-401 status regardless. Best practice — OWASP, and GitLab's
    // production shared-IP Git-over-HTTPS limiter — is to gate failures, not
    // total traffic, and not to throttle correct-credential requests by IP.)

    // Classify the route: WebSocket-upgrade (path "/v1/signal") versus
    // plain HTTP. The strict surface for each is then enforced.
    let is_ws_upgrade = req.uri().path() == "/v1/signal";

    let header_token: Option<Vec<u8>> = req.headers()
        .get(axum::http::header::AUTHORIZATION)
        .and_then(|v| v.to_str().ok())
        .and_then(|s| s.strip_prefix("Bearer "))
        .map(|s| s.as_bytes().to_vec());

    let query_token: Option<Vec<u8>> = req.uri().query().and_then(|q| {
        url::form_urlencoded::parse(q.as_bytes())
            .find(|(k, _)| k == "token")
            .map(|(_, v)| v.into_owned().into_bytes())
    });

    // STRICT: reject the wrong-surface presentation explicitly. No silent
    // acceptance from the alternative source.
    let presented_token: Vec<u8> = if is_ws_upgrade {
        if header_token.is_some() {
            return Err((axum::http::StatusCode::BAD_REQUEST,
                "auth: token must be in ?token= query parameter for WebSocket \
                 upgrade; Authorization header rejected".to_string()));
        }
        match query_token {
            Some(t) => t,
            None => return Err((axum::http::StatusCode::UNAUTHORIZED,
                "auth: missing ?token= query parameter on WebSocket upgrade".to_string())),
        }
    } else {
        if query_token.is_some() {
            return Err((axum::http::StatusCode::BAD_REQUEST,
                "auth: token must be in Authorization header for HTTP routes; \
                 query parameter rejected".to_string()));
        }
        match header_token {
            Some(t) => t,
            None => return Err((axum::http::StatusCode::UNAUTHORIZED,
                "auth: missing Authorization: Bearer header".to_string())),
        }
    };

    use subtle::ConstantTimeEq;
    let ok = bool::from(presented_token.ct_eq(state.auth_secret.as_bytes()));
    if !ok {
        // FAILED auth only: charge ONE token to this IP's brute-force budget.
        // Once the budget is empty, further WRONG-token attempts get 429 instead
        // of 401 — capping a guesser at the same ~7200 failed attempts/day/IP the
        // quota intends, while NEVER touching correct-token traffic (so shared-NAT
        // viewers are never throttled). governor's RateLimiter has no peek/dry-run
        // (verified, 0.10.4 src/state/keyed.rs:145), so calling check_key only
        // here — not before the compare — is exactly what makes success free.
        if let Err(not_until) = state.auth_rate_limiter.check_key(&ip) {
            use governor::clock::Clock;
            let wait_secs = not_until.wait_time_from(governor::clock::DefaultClock::default().now())
                .as_secs().max(1);
            let mut resp = (
                axum::http::StatusCode::TOO_MANY_REQUESTS,
                format!("rate_limited: try again in {wait_secs}s")
            ).into_response();
            resp.headers_mut().insert(
                axum::http::header::RETRY_AFTER,
                axum::http::HeaderValue::from(wait_secs)
            );
            return Ok(resp);
        }
        return Err((axum::http::StatusCode::UNAUTHORIZED,
            "auth: invalid token".to_string()));
    }
    Ok(next.run(req).await)
}

Symmetric discipline on the WebAssembly client side: HTTP fetches set the Authorization: Bearer header via RequestInit::headers; the signaling WebSocket constructor uses the ?token=<secret> form exclusively. The client does not present the token in both places "to be safe" — that would defeat the rejection path above.

17.6 axum-server bootstrap with TLS, ConnectInfo, and graceful shutdown

Critical correction (verified by source-level research on 2026-06-01). Earlier drafts of this plan mixed axum_server::tls_rustls::RustlsConfig (Section 14.7) with axum::serve(listener, app).with_graceful_shutdown(...). Those two paths do not compose — axum::serve is plain TCP and does not accept a RustlsConfig; axum_server has its own listening / serving machinery built on raw hyper, with only a dev-dependency on axum 0.7 (not a runtime dep). The correct pattern is to commit fully to axum-server's bind_rustls path AND its Handle::graceful_shutdown(Some(Duration)) for shutdown signaling. This is the path the plan now uses end-to-end. 
use anyhow::Context;
use axum_server::Handle;
use axum_server::tls_rustls::RustlsConfig;
use std::{net::SocketAddr, time::Duration};

// rustls_config constructed by tls::build_rustls_config (cert+key load, TLS 1.3 only).
let rustls_config: RustlsConfig = /* ... */;

// Construct the axum Router with state and middleware (auth + security headers + trace).
let app: axum::Router = router.layer(/* middleware */).with_state(state.clone());

// The Handle is the bridge between the signal-watcher task and the serve loop.
// `axum_server::Handle::graceful_shutdown(Option)` is verified at
// https://docs.rs/axum-server/0.8.0/src/axum_server/handle.rs.html#70.
// Cloning Handle is cheap (it's an Arc-wrapped inner shared state).
let handle = Handle::new();

// Spawn the signal-watcher task that drives graceful shutdown. The
// shutdown signal is the shared Notify in AppState; the watcher awaits
// it once and then triggers axum-server's graceful_shutdown.
let shutdown_handle = handle.clone();
let shutdown_notify = state.shutdown.clone();      // Arc<tokio::sync::Notify>
tokio::spawn(async move {
    shutdown_notify.notified().await;
    // 3 s graceful budget; the per-connection HTTP drain composes with this.
    shutdown_handle.graceful_shutdown(Some(Duration::from_secs(3)));
});

// bind_rustls expects an explicit SocketAddr (not a pre-bound TcpListener).
// IPv4-only: 127.0.0.1:8443 by default, 0.0.0.0:8443 with --bind-all-interfaces.
// No IPv6 socket is ever opened (the binary refuses IPv6 --public-host values).
let bind_addr: SocketAddr = /* IPv4-only; see config.rs for the construction */;

// TCP keepalive on accepted signaling sockets (Section 17.6.1 below).
// We need the per-stream sockopt to be applied between accept() and the
// TLS+HTTP machinery. axum-server's `Acceptor` trait is the seam.
let acceptor = axum_server::tls_rustls::RustlsAcceptor::new(rustls_config)
    .acceptor(KeepaliveAcceptor::default());   // 0.8.0: the inner-acceptor setter is `.acceptor(...)`, NOT `with_inner`

axum_server::bind(bind_addr)
    .handle(handle)
    .acceptor(acceptor)
    .serve(app.into_make_service_with_connect_info::<SocketAddr>())
    .await
    .context("axum_server::bind serve")?;

Important details verified at the pinned versions on 2026-06-01:

  • axum_server::bind(SocketAddr).acceptor(A).serve(...) is the long-form of bind_rustls that lets us inject a custom Acceptor wrapped around the rustls TLS acceptor. Per axum-server 0.8.0 src/tls_rustls/mod.rs:147-155, RustlsAcceptor::acceptor(inner) overrides the inner acceptor (there is no with_inner in 0.8.0); RustlsAcceptor::accept runs that inner acceptor on the RAW tokio::net::TcpStream FIRST and only then starts the TLS handshake (src/tls_rustls/future.rs:77-85), so our KeepaliveAcceptor sets the socket options pre-TLS on the bare socket and they persist for the connection's TlsStream<TcpStream> lifetime.
  • .handle(Handle) attaches a shutdown-control handle.
  • .serve(make_service) takes any Tower MakeService that produces a per-connection axum Service. app.into_make_service_with_connect_info::<SocketAddr>() from axum 0.8.9 (verified at /tmp/axum-src/axum/src/routing/mod.rs:537) is exactly such a MakeService; the ConnectInfo<SocketAddr> extractor used in auth_layer (Section 17.5) reads the per-connection peer address from this.
  • UDP port 51000 (own-STUN + WebRTC media) is handled by a separate tokio::net::UdpSocket in the Selective Forwarding Unit actor task (Section 11.3); it does not touch axum-server.
  • Handle::graceful_shutdown(Some(Duration::from_secs(3))): per /tmp/axum-server-src/src/handle.rs:70, this notifies in-flight connections to drain within the given budget. Per Section 18, the outer wrapper at process level allows up to 8 s total; this inner budget plus the per-connection 3 s HTTP drain composes cleanly.

17.6.1 TCP keepalive on accepted signaling sockets — defense against zombie WS handlers

An accepted signaling WebSocket TCP connection that dies mid-stream (router cycle, peer's tab crashed, peer's wifi card died) leaves the corresponding axum WebSocket-handler task parked on socket.recv().await until Linux's default tcp_keepalive_time of 7200 seconds fires (verified at man 7 tcp). With many viewers, that's two hours of leaked tokio tasks holding stack frames, mpsc Receivers, and per-peer FSM bookkeeping. Apply TCP_KEEPALIVE + TCP_USER_TIMEOUT to every accepted stream so a dead peer is culled in ~25 seconds, matching the LA WebSocket defense in Section 19.4.1.1.

use std::time::Duration;
use std::io;

use axum_server::accept::Accept;
use socket2::{SockRef, TcpKeepalive};
use tokio::net::TcpStream;

/// Acceptor that applies TCP_KEEPALIVE + TCP_USER_TIMEOUT to every accepted
/// stream, then passes it through. Same 10/5/3 + 25 s parameters as the LA WS
/// socket (Section 19.4.1.1), so the maximum time from peer-vanish to
/// handler-task reaped is bounded at ~25 s. Modelled on axum-server 0.8.0's
/// built-in NoDelayAcceptor (src/accept.rs:46-65): concrete TcpStream, generic
/// service S, mutate the sockopt, return Ready.
#[derive(Default, Clone, Copy)]
pub struct KeepaliveAcceptor;

impl<S> Accept<TcpStream, S> for KeepaliveAcceptor {
    // axum-server 0.8.0 Accept trait (src/accept.rs:11-23). The service slot is
    // the concrete per-connection value (generic `S`); `SendService` is a SEALED
    // BOUND that `serve` applies (src/server.rs:267-269), NOT a nameable type — so
    // it must NOT appear here (the earlier `type Service = SendService` did not
    // compile). RustlsAcceptor forwards the raw TcpStream to this inner acceptor
    // BEFORE TLS (§17.6), so `stream` is the bare socket and socket2 can set
    // keepalive on it; the option then persists on the resulting TlsStream.
    type Stream  = TcpStream;
    type Service = S;
    type Future  = std::future::Ready<io::Result<(TcpStream, S)>>;

    fn accept(&self, stream: TcpStream, service: S) -> Self::Future {
        // ACTUALLY apply keepalive here (the earlier pass-through never did, so
        // the zombie-reaping guarantee was silently absent). Fail loud (§3): map
        // any sockopt error to io::Error so the connection is dropped rather than
        // served without keepalive.
        let res = apply_keepalive(&stream).map(|()| (stream, service));
        std::future::ready(res)
    }
}

/// Apply TCP_KEEPALIVE + TCP_USER_TIMEOUT to a raw accepted TcpStream (pre-TLS).
fn apply_keepalive(tcp: &TcpStream) -> io::Result<()> {
    let sock_ref = SockRef::from(tcp);   // From<&S: AsFd>; tokio TcpStream: AsFd on unix

    // Idle 10 s, probe every 5 s, fail after 3 unanswered probes → ~25 s
    // worst-case dead-peer detection. Identical to Section 19.4.1.1 for symmetry.
    // NOTE: with_retries() AND set_tcp_user_timeout() are gated behind socket2's
    // `all` feature (socket2 0.6.4 src/lib.rs:556, src/sys/unix.rs:2528) — the
    // socket2 pin in §7.4 MUST carry features = ["all"] or this does not compile.
    let keepalive = TcpKeepalive::new()
        .with_time(Duration::from_secs(10))
        .with_interval(Duration::from_secs(5))
        .with_retries(3);
    sock_ref.set_tcp_keepalive(&keepalive)
        .map_err(|e| io::Error::other(format!("set_tcp_keepalive on signaling WS socket: {e}")))?;
    sock_ref.set_tcp_user_timeout(Some(Duration::from_secs(25)))
        .map_err(|e| io::Error::other(format!("set_tcp_user_timeout on signaling WS socket: {e}")))?;
    Ok(())
}

Properties:

  • Symmetric with the LA path (Section 19.4.1.1). Same 10/5/3 keepalive, same 25-second TCP_USER_TIMEOUT. No design surface duplication.
  • Catches the 5 AM router cycle. When the LAN switch loses power for 90 s, the per-peer WS handler's recv() on a dead TCP connection returns ETIMEDOUT at ~25 s post-disconnect (not Linux's 7200 s default). The chain propagates FsmCommand::Disconnect { peer_id, reason, intentional: false } to the FSM (a router-cycle TCP timeout is an unintentional drop); the WS handler returns; tokio reclaims the task. No 50-viewer task leak.
  • Application-level SignalMessage::Keepalive {} every 15 s (Section 11.2.1) remains the FSM-side keepalive timer. The TCP keepalive here is transport-level defense; the application keepalive ensures the FSM treats application-silent peers as gone within 30 s even when TCP appears healthy (e.g., wifi-portal MITM that ACKs TCP but drops application bytes).

18. Graceful Shutdown Sequence #

Docker's default SIGTERM-to-SIGKILL grace period is 10 seconds. The full shutdown sequence must complete within approximately 8 seconds to leave a 2-second buffer.

18.1 Trigger

SIGINT (operator Control-C) or SIGTERM (Docker stop). Both handled equally; whichever fires first wins. The main task awaits both via tokio::select! { _ = tokio::signal::ctrl_c() => ..., _ = tokio::signal::unix::signal(SignalKind::terminate())?.recv() => ... }.

18.2 Sequence

  1. Signal received → state.shutdown.notify_waiters() on the shared Arc<tokio::sync::Notify>. Time budget: under 10 milliseconds.
  2. The signal-watcher task (Section 17.6) calls handle.graceful_shutdown(Some(Duration::from_secs(3))) on the axum_server::Handle. The accept loop breaks → no new HTTP connections, no new WebSocket upgrades. Time budget: under 50 milliseconds.
  3. In parallel:
    • Per-connection HTTP handlers: complete in-flight requests within the 3-second budget axum-server enforces via the Handle.
    • Per-connection WebSocket handlers: observe shutdown.notified(), send SignalMessage::Bye, then a WebSocket Close frame with code 1001 (Going Away), then return from the handler task.
    • FSM actor task: receives FsmCommand::Shutdown. For each peer in self.clients: write a DataChannel "bye" message via str0m::channel::Channel::write, then call poll_output repeatedly until the DataChannel send buffer drains (capped at 100 milliseconds per peer), then call rtc.disconnect() on the Rtc. All peers in parallel via futures::future::join_all wrapped in tokio::time::timeout(Duration::from_millis(800), ...).
    • LocateAnything-3B WebSocket client task: call WebSocketStream::close(Some(CloseFrame { code: 1000.into(), reason: "".into() })).await, then loop while ws.next().await.is_some() wrapped in tokio::time::timeout(Duration::from_millis(500), ...) to drain the peer's close response.
    Combined time budget: 3 seconds (HTTP drain dominates, capped by axum-server's Handle::graceful_shutdown budget).
  4. The axum_server::bind_rustls(...).serve(...).await future returns once every per-connection task has dropped its handle. This typically returns very quickly because the per-connection HTTP task ends at the WebSocket upgrade boundary; upgraded WebSocket tasks have already dropped their handles in step 3.
  5. Outer wrapper: tokio::time::timeout(Duration::from_secs(8), serve_future). On elapse, log a WARN line and continue (the underlying handler tasks have all been notified and will drain themselves; we just stop waiting). Time budget: 8 seconds.
  6. Join the long-lived tasks (deterministic, not a blind sleep). tokio::time::timeout(Duration::from_secs(1), futures::future::join_all([sfu_h, la_h])).await (the two tokio tasks), then join the decode OS thread via tokio::task::spawn_blocking(move || decode_thread.join()) within the same budget. run_sfu's select loop intercepts FsmCommand::Shutdown, runs the async on_shutdown (per-peer DataChannel "bye" flush + rtc.disconnect(), step 3, ≤800 ms) and then breaks — so sfu_h resolves; la_h returns on its WS close; the decode pipeline OS thread (§11.3) returns as soon as the SFU drops au_tx (its blocking_recv() yields None) and emits no DataChannel byes itself, so joining it is best-effort cleanup with no shutdown-ordering obligation. Because on_shutdown's flush begins at signal time (step 2) and overlaps the serve-drain, by the time this join runs the tasks have typically already returned, so it completes near-instantly; only a pathological stall waits out the 1 s cap (then log a WARN and exit anyway — the handlers were already notified). This replaces the former blind 1-second sleep, closing the race where a bye-flush slower than the sleep let the process exit before the browser saw its clean goodbye (§18.3). Time budget: ≤1 second, sequential to step 5 — worst-case total shutdown is 8 s (step 5's serve cap) + 1 s = 9 s, preserving a 1-second buffer under the Docker 10-second SIGTERM→SIGKILL window (§15.7.1); the realistic path (serve returns sub-second, tasks already joined) is well under 3 s.
  7. Process exits with code 0.

18.3 Critical str0m disconnect detail

str0m::Rtc::disconnect does only self.alive = false (verified at str0m-src/src/lib.rs:1267-1272). It does NOT send a Real-time Transport Control Protocol Bye message, NOT a Datagram Transport Layer Security close_notify, NOT a STUN goodbye. After disconnect() is called, poll_output returns Output::Timeout(not_happening()) and handle_input discards input. This means: to actually deliver a goodbye to the browser, the server MUST send a DataChannel "bye" payload AND pump poll_output (so the User Datagram Protocol packet leaves the network interface) BEFORE calling disconnect(). The browser then sees the application-level "bye", and the browser's own RTCPeerConnection.close() will issue a clean DTLS close_notify on the browser's side. Without this sequence, the browser learns of disconnect only via ICE consent-freshness timeout, which is approximately 5 seconds (per RFC 7675 default) to approximately 30 seconds (browser-implementation-specific).

19. Server-Side Video Pipeline #

19.1 Pipeline diagram — natural-pace sampling driven by LocateAnything-3B's stop-and-wait cadence

Sampling cadence: LocateAnything-3B's stop-and-wait latency is the natural rate. The LocateAnything-3B server enforces strict one-frame-in / one-reply-out per WebSocket connection — verified at /home/user/Desktop/locate_anything_setup/docs/CLIENT_PROTOCOL.md:14 ("strictly stop-and-wait per connection") and in code at /home/user/Desktop/locate_anything_setup/worker/la_worker.py:168, 438-444 (an asyncio.Lock() held for the entire forward pass). Measured per-frame inference latency per /home/user/Desktop/locate_anything_setup/docs/DRONE_DETECTION.md:113-117: ~785 ms hybrid on 1080p (~1.30 fps), ~937 ms slow (~1.07 fps), ~210 ms for the point_to calibration target (~4.8 fps). The demo server's LA-side rate is whatever the per-frame latency happens to be — no scheduler, no hardcoded interval, no artificial delay anywhere in the pipeline.

The pipeline is structured so that LocateAnything-3B naturally pulls a frame at its own pace:

  1. SFU → decoder feed (non-coalescing). The Selective Forwarding Unit's on_streamer_media_data handler does two things on every Event::MediaData from the streamer: (a) re-publish the access unit (AU) to every viewer via str0m::Writer (Section 11.7, unchanged); (b) push the AU into a bounded tokio::sync::mpsc::channel(256) toward the decode-pipeline thread. The capacity is sized in frames, not seconds — the producer-side framerate is whatever the browser encoder happens to be emitting (the WebRTC capture target is 30 fps but libwebrtc's QualityScaler may drop it under mobile thermal or battery-save throttle); 256 access units is a generous safety margin against any transient decoder stall regardless of the live encoder rate. In-order delivery, no coalescing. The H.264 reference chain across P-frames is preserved end-to-end. On try_send returning Err(Full), the SFU surfaces an anyhow::Error up to its supervising task, which disconnects the streamer per the strict no-fallback rule (Section 3); a chronic Full condition indicates the decode pipeline has wedged and silent backpressure would only mask the bug.
  2. Decoder (stateful, long-lived). One ffmpeg_next::codec::context::Context with an open H.264 decoder lives for the entire streaming session. The thread calls decoder.send_packet(&packet) and decoder.receive_frame(&mut yuv) per AU. libavcodec naturally suppresses output before the H.264 recovery flag is set (verified at upstream libavcodec/h264_slice.c:1376-1383: without AV_CODEC_FLAG_OUTPUT_CORRUPT, output is held until out->recovered = true, set on IDR at line 1664 or on an SEI recovery-point message). Therefore: a fresh decoder attaching mid-stream on a P-frame DOES NOT emit corrupt pixels — it stays silent until the recovery IDR arrives. Cold-start before the very first IDR is correct by construction. Mid-stream Sequence Parameter Set changes (e.g., the streamer's camera changing resolution via RTCRtpSender.replaceTrack in Section 21.6) are handled by libavcodec's automatic re-init path at h264_slice.c:1080-1090.
  3. Packet-loss recovery is event-driven. str0m's depacketizer holds packets for up to 30 frames (~1 second at 30 fps; default at str0m 0.20.0 src/config.rs:639) waiting for Negative-Acknowledgement-driven retransmission via the 3-second StreamTx cache (src/streams/send.rs:38, 275). When that hold-back window expires without recovery, str0m emits MediaData.contiguous = false; the SFU forwards that flag to the decode thread on the AU channel itself (as part of the DecodeAu payload — Section 11.7 — so it stays ordered with the access unit it describes). The decode thread, on seeing contiguous == false, calls decoder.flush() to clear the Decoded Picture Buffer AND signals the FSM (blocking_send, since the thread is off-runtime) to emit one KeyframeRequestKind::Pli upstream to the streamer. This is the str0m reference pattern (examples/chat.rs:442-445) verbatim — event-driven, not periodic.
  4. YUV → RGB conversion (long-lived swscale Context). Each successfully-decoded YUV420P frame goes through libswscale via ffmpeg_next::software::scaling::Context with Flags::BICUBIC and an explicit sws_setColorspaceDetails call (BT.709 source coefficients, limited→full range — see Section 19.3 for the unsafe FFI shape and rationale). The swscale Context is long-lived across the streaming session; it is reconstructed only when the decoded frame's dimensions change (the libavcodec auto-reinit signal at h264_slice.c:1080-1090).
  5. Decoder → LA feed (coalescing safe). Each RGB frame is published to a tokio::sync::watch::Sender<Option<RgbFrame>>. The watch's 1-slot coalescing semantic is safe here because each RGB frame is self-contained — no cross-frame state, no reference chain. The LA WebSocket client task is the sole consumer.
  6. LA WebSocket client (the natural-rate driver). The canonical Tokio watch idiom drives a strict stop-and-wait loop: 
    loop {
    jpeg_rx.changed().await
        .context("decoder pipeline closed unexpectedly")?;
    let frame = jpeg_rx.borrow_and_update().clone();
    let jpeg  = jpeg_encode(&frame)
        .with_context(|| format!("mozjpeg encode (size={}x{})", frame.width, frame.height))?;
    let wire  = build_la_wire(&header, &jpeg);
    ws.send(Message::Binary(wire.into())).await
        .context("LA WS send")?;
    let reply = ws.next().await
        .ok_or_else(|| anyhow::anyhow!("LA closed mid-frame"))??;
    dispatch_la_reply(reply).await?;
}
    changed().await parks until at least one new frame has been published since the last call. borrow_and_update() returns the freshest frame and marks it seen. By the time LA has replied (~785 ms hybrid), the decoder has typically overwritten the watch slot 20+ times; the next changed() resolves immediately with the latest. LA's natural latency is the cadence. No periodic scheduler, no tokio::time::interval, no hardcoded number.

PLI emission is fully event-driven; no application-level throttle is needed. Two structural rate-caps already sit below the SFU's application code:

  • str0m's pending_request_keyframe: Option<KeyframeRequestKind> field at src/streams/receive.rs:80 is single-slot: request_keyframe() at :221-222 ASSIGNS (= Some(kind)), so multiple calls within one drain window collapse to the most recent. The field is drained inside Session::handle_timeout (src/session.rs:226-258); the dominant recurring wakeup is the 50-millisecond TWCC_INTERVAL at src/session.rs:42, so the wall-clock cap on outbound PLI is ≤ one per ~50 ms.
  • libwebrtc's kMinKeyframeSendIntervalMs = 300 at video/encoder_rtcp_feedback.cc:23-24 drops any PLI received within 300 ms of the last emitted IDR — the streamer's encoder physically cannot produce IDRs faster than ~3.3 / second regardless of the SFU's request rate.

An application-level throttle on top of these two structural floors would be a third redundant layer with no behavioral effect; we omit it to honor the no-overengineered-options principle of Section 3. New-viewer attach (Section 11.7) and the !contiguous recovery path are the only PLI emitters.

str0m streamer Rtc instance
  └── on Event::MediaData { data: Arc<[u8]>, time: MediaTime, contiguous: bool, .. }
        (data is an Annex-B framed H.264 access unit with SPS+PPS prepended on
         IDRs; the H.264 depacketizer at /tmp/str0m-src/src/packet/h264.rs:317,350,387
         emits start-code-framed bytes regardless of how the RTP packets were
         aggregated on the wire. STAP-A aggregations and FU-A fragments both
         collapse into Annex-B in the same data buffer.)
        │
        ▼
SFU actor (on_streamer_media_data):
  - Always re-publish to viewers via str0m::Writer (Section 11.7).
  - Push every AU into mpsc::channel(256) toward the decode thread. On Err(Full),
    propagate anyhow::Error up; supervising task disconnects the streamer.
        │
        ▼
Decode pipeline — DEDICATED OS THREAD (owns the libavcodec H.264 Context AND
the !Send libswscale Context). Sync fn run via std::thread (Section 11.3):
  fn run_decode_pipeline(
      mut au_rx: tokio::sync::mpsc::Receiver<DecodeAu>,
      rgb_tx:    tokio::sync::watch::Sender<Option<RgbFrame>>,
      fsm_tx:    tokio::sync::mpsc::Sender<FsmCommand>,
  ) -> anyhow::Result<()> {
      use ffmpeg_next::codec::{Context as CodecContext, Id as CodecId};
      // Built once at thread entry; live for the whole streaming session.
      let mut decoder = CodecContext::new().decoder().open_as(CodecId::H264)
          .context("opening H.264 decoder via libavcodec")?;
      let mut scaler_slot: Option<ffmpeg_next::software::scaling::Context> = None; // rebuilt on dim change (ensure_scaler_for, §19.3)
      let mut rgb_frame = ffmpeg_next::frame::Video::empty();   // RGB24 output scratch (reused)
      let mut yuv       = ffmpeg_next::frame::Video::empty();   // decoder output scratch (reused)
      loop {
          // blocking_recv bridges the async SFU (tokio mpsc) to this sync thread.
          // None = the SFU dropped au_tx (shutdown) → return so main can join us.
          let Some(au) = au_rx.blocking_recv() else { return Ok(()); };
          if !au.contiguous {
              // str0m's depacketizer exhausted its NACK/RTX hold-back without
              // recovering a lost packet. Flush the DPB — stale references would
              // corrupt every dependent P-frame — and ask the FSM to PLI the
              // streamer (Section 20.6). blocking_send, NOT .await: this is a
              // plain OS thread, never a runtime worker. (str0m chat.rs:442-445.)
              decoder.flush();
              fsm_tx.blocking_send(FsmCommand::RequestStreamerPli)
                  .context("FSM channel closed (RequestStreamerPli)")?;
          }
          let packet = ffmpeg_next::packet::Packet::copy(&au.data);
          decoder.send_packet(&packet)
              .with_context(|| format!("libavcodec send_packet (len={})", au.data.len()))?;
          match decoder.receive_frame(&mut yuv) {            // yuv: frame::Video (reused scratch)
              Ok(()) => {
                  if yuv.is_corrupt() {                       // AV_FRAME_FLAG_CORRUPT — extra-defensive
                      tracing::warn!("libavcodec emitted corrupt frame; dropping");
                      continue;
                  }
              }
              Err(ffmpeg_next::Error::Other { errno })
                  if errno == ffmpeg_next::error::EAGAIN => continue, // cold-start, benign (h264_slice.c:1376-1383)
              Err(e) => return Err(e).context("libavcodec receive_frame"),
          }
          // Colour-convert this frame and publish. We convert EVERY decoded frame
          // (not just the ones LA consumes): on this hardware the swscale of the
          // ~29 frames/sec LA skips is irrelevant, and gating it on an LA-demand
          // flag would be cross-thread state to keep in sync for no real saving
          // (Section 3 — no overengineering). The 1-slot watch coalesces, so LA
          // still samples only the freshest frame at its own pace.
          ensure_scaler_for(&mut scaler_slot, yuv.width(), yuv.height())?;
          scaler_slot.as_mut().unwrap().run(&yuv, &mut rgb_frame)
              .context("libswscale run YUV420P→RGB24")?;
          rgb_tx.send(Some(rgb_frame.clone()))
              .context("rgb watch channel closed")?;
      }
  }
        │
        ▼
LA WebSocket client task (the natural-rate driver):
  loop {
      jpeg_rx.changed().await?;                          // park if no new frame
      let frame   = jpeg_rx.borrow_and_update().clone(); // latest wins
      let jpeg    = mozjpeg_encode_q92(&frame)?;         // see Section 19.2
      let header  = LaInferHeader { frame_id, prompt, generation_mode,
                                    jpeg_len: jpeg.len() };
      let mut wire = Vec::with_capacity(4 + header_json.len() + jpeg.len());
      wire.extend_from_slice(&(header_json.len() as u32).to_be_bytes());
      wire.extend_from_slice(&header_json);
      wire.extend_from_slice(&jpeg);
      ws.send(Message::Binary(wire.into())).await?;
      let reply = ws.next().await
          .ok_or_else(|| anyhow!("LA closed mid-frame"))??;
      // Dispatch reply to FSM (Section 11.7.1's fan-out path):
      //   type:"result" -> FsmCommand::LaBoxResult { msg: BoxMessage }
      //   type:"error"  -> FsmCommand::LaErrorResult { frame_id, code, message }
  }

End-to-end fps under this design, using the documented 1080p LA latency as a conservative upper bound for cycle time (720p inputs will execute somewhat faster due to fewer Vision Transformer tokens, but the actual 720p latency is unmeasured — see Section 27):

LA generation modeLA inference timePipeline overhead (mozjpeg + libswscale + LAN WS RTT)End-to-end cyclefps
Hybrid~785 ms~10–15 ms~800 ms~1.25
Slow~937 ms~10–15 ms~950 ms~1.05
Point (calibration only)~210 ms~10–15 ms~225 ms~4.4

Critical-path overhead (single x86_64 modern core, target host being the Intel Core Ultra 9 285K per Section 1; on-host measurement is the gap recorded in Section 27, but the bracket below is consistent with published benchmarks):

  • mozjpeg encode at Q92 baseline 4:2:0, knobs disabled per Section 19.2: ~5–10 ms at 720p (~0.92 megapixels).
  • libswscale BICUBIC YUV420P → RGB24 at 720p: ~3–5 ms.
  • WebSocket send + receive over LAN: ~0.5–1 ms (1 Gbps switch, ~50–80 KiB JPEG payload at Q92).
  • JSON reply parse + DataChannel fan-out + tokio scheduling: <0.5 ms total.

The decode pipeline runs on a dedicated OS thread (Sections 11.3 and 12.4 — deliberately NOT one of the 4 tokio workers: the libswscale Context is !Send, and a persistent CPU-bound loop should not occupy an async worker): at 30 fps, ~6–12 ms per 720p H.264 software-decode frame plus the libswscale step puts the decode thread at roughly 18–36 % of one core sustained. We convert every decoded frame rather than gating colour-conversion on LA's cadence: the swscale of the frames LA skips costs a small fraction of one core and is not worth a cross-thread demand flag to keep in sync (Section 3 — no overengineering), and the 1-slot watch already coalesces so LA samples only the freshest. Well within budget. The LA client task's critical path sees only its own per-cycle overhead — the mozjpeg encode of the single frame it consumes — because the decode thread runs fully in parallel; the ~2 % fps loss versus LA's native throughput (hybrid mode) is the dominant accounting term and is invariant to streamer-side frame rate.

19.2 mozjpeg knob discipline

mozjpeg::Compress::new() silently selects the JCP_MAX_COMPRESSION profile (per mozjpeg-rust-src/src/compress.rs:99-103 and the underlying C library mozjpeg-research/jcparam.c:498-500), which enables progressive JPEG, multipass optimize_scans search, and trellis quantization. This is the approximately 45-times-slower-than-libjpeg-turbo path. The implementation agent must explicitly call:
  • compress.set_optimize_scans(false) — falls back to baseline JPEG (also nulls cinfo.scan_info); approximately 45x speedup.
  • compress.set_use_scans_in_trellis(false) — already false by default but be explicit.
  • compress.set_smoothing_factor(0) — disables edge-smoothing pre-pass; smoothing hurts detection edge crispness.
Result: approximately 8 to 15 milliseconds per 720p frame at quality 92 on a typical x86_64 server core. mozjpeg encode sits on the per-frame critical path that the LA WebSocket client task drains — adding ~10 ms to whatever the upstream inference latency is. Even against LocateAnything-3B's fastest mode (point_to at ~210 ms per docs/DRONE_DETECTION.md:113-117) that is < 5 % of the cycle; against hybrid (~785 ms) it is < 2 %. The design tolerates much faster models too — if a future LocateAnything variant runs at ~50 ms inference, mozjpeg becomes ~20 % of the cycle, still acceptable.

19.3 libswscale colorspace — BT.709 limited→full applied explicitly

libswscale defaults to BT.601 (Recommendation BT.601 from the International Telecommunication Union) for unspecified colorspace input. Webcam-sourced H.264 overwhelmingly signals BT.709 in its Video Usability Information (VUI) parameters across the browser matrix (modern device convention), but str0m::media::MediaData does NOT pass VUI through. The implementation therefore reaches through Context::as_mut_ptr() to call sws_setColorspaceDetails with BT.709 source coefficients (SWS_CS_ITU709) + limited (TV) source range + full (PC) RGB destination range. See the code block above in Section 19.1.

Why this matters for detection quality, and why it does NOT defeat the JFIF round-trip property: the LocateAnything-3B worker (worker/inference.py) decodes incoming JPEGs through PIL with full-range RGB output, and the worker explicitly assumes the model is trained on sRGB inputs (sRGB shares the same primaries as BT.709, not BT.601). The mozjpeg encode step further down the pipeline applies BT.601 full-range YCbCr coefficients internally (JFIF default) and the worker's PIL decode applies the matching inverse — that round-trip is matrix-consistent on its own and is independent of which matrix we used at the YUV→RGB step. The earlier "BT.601 matches JFIF default" reasoning conflated those two layers; sourcing the RGB tensor via BT.709 (matching the actual camera-produced source YUV) is strictly more correct because the JFIF round-trip on the downstream side does not care which matrix produced the RGB it sees.

Cost: zero on the per-frame fast path. sws_setColorspaceDetails is a one-time setup call per Context construction (which is itself per-dimension-change); the libswscale SIMD kernels selected are identical regardless of the matrix coefficients. Verified against ffmpeg-sys-next's exposed signature and the FFmpeg cvslog guidance that the call is most-reliable after sws_init_context/Context::get().

19.4 LocateAnything-3B WebSocket client reconnect

The WebSocket connection to the LocateAnything-3B server can drop (LocateAnything-3B server restart, transient network blip on the LAN, the 05:00 router cycle). The client task wraps tokio_tungstenite::connect_async plus the send-loop in an outer loop with exponential-backoff retry: 500 milliseconds, 1 second, 2 seconds, 4 seconds, 8 seconds, capped at 10 seconds. No mid-frame retry; no buffering.

Keepalive ping during long Idle periods. The LocateAnything-3B server enforces a 60-second read-idle close (verified at /home/user/Desktop/locate_anything_setup/docs/CLIENT_PROTOCOL.md:294 — Close code 1001 "server shutdown / 60 s read-idle"). The demo server's default state is Idle (no streamer), during which no frames are sent to LocateAnything-3B; without explicit keepalive the LA server's read-idle timer fires and silently 1001s the connection, then the first frame of the next streamer claim hits a reconnect-storm that drops ~500-2000 ms of streamer video. The LA WebSocket client task therefore sends a RFC 6455 Message::Ping(Bytes::new()) on a 30-second interval while not actively sending frames. RFC 6455 Pings/Pongs are handled transparently by both tokio-tungstenite (sender side) and the LA server's underlying axum WS handler (receiver side); no application-level pong-tracking code is needed. Implementation shape:

let mut keepalive = tokio::time::interval(Duration::from_secs(30));
keepalive.set_missed_tick_behavior(tokio::time::MissedTickBehavior::Skip);
loop {
    tokio::select! {
        biased;
        // Note: non-refutable binding. If the watch Sender is dropped (decoder
        // task gone), `changed()` returns Err(RecvError) and propagates via `?`
        // — the LA task exits loud. A refutable `Ok(()) = ...` pattern would
        // SILENTLY DISABLE the branch on close (per tokio's select! macro at
        // src/macros/select.rs:46, 85-87) and the task would hang waiting on
        // the next keepalive tick — exactly the fail-silent mode Section 3
        // forbids.
        result = jpeg_rx.changed() => {
            result.context("decoder pipeline closed unexpectedly")?;
            let frame = jpeg_rx.borrow_and_update().clone();
            send_frame(&mut ws, frame).await
                .context("send JPEG frame to LA")?;
        }
        _ = keepalive.tick() => {
            ws.send(tokio_tungstenite::tungstenite::Message::Ping(Default::default()))
                .await
                .context("LA WS keepalive ping")?;
        }
    }
}

The MissedTickBehavior::Skip setting prevents a backlog of keepalives queueing up after a long busy period (otherwise an interval that was suppressed for ten ticks would fire ten times in rapid succession). The biased selector prefers frame-send over ping, so a steadily-streaming session never emits a keepalive.

19.4.1 Two-layer defense against unresponsive LocateAnything-3B — TCP-dead AND app-hang

A non-responsive LocateAnything-3B comes in two distinct failure modes, each requiring a different defense:

  1. TCP-dead peer. Cable yanked, container crashed, kernel panicked, route blackholed. The remote IP stack is no longer ACKing packets. Detection mechanism: OS-level TCP_KEEPALIVE probes (Linux tcp_keepalive_time/tcp_keepalive_intvl/tcp_keepalive_probes, also reachable via SO_KEEPALIVE + TCP_KEEPIDLE/TCP_KEEPINTVL/TCP_KEEPCNT) plus TCP_USER_TIMEOUT (RFC 5482, Linux-specific) for bounded transmission-stall on outbound bytes.
  2. App-hung peer. LocateAnything-3B process is alive (CUDA kernel stuck, Python GIL deadlock, GPU OOM-stall, etc.) but the kernel's TCP stack is fine and ACKs everything. Keepalive probes succeed. TCP_USER_TIMEOUT does not engage because there are no outbound bytes waiting on ACK — our last ws.send finished, the LA-side kernel buffered the bytes, and we are now in ws.next().await. Detection of this case requires an application-level bound on how long we wait for the WebSocket reply.

The plan applies both defenses simultaneously. They are orthogonal and cover orthogonal failure modes.

19.4.1.1 Defense layer 1 — Linux TCP keepalive on the LocateAnything-3B socket

Catches TCP-dead. Transport-level, latency-agnostic — the parameters do not change if LocateAnything-3B is replaced by a model with 10 ms inference or 10 s inference; they detect whether the remote TCP stack is alive, not whether the model is fast.

use std::time::Duration;
use socket2::{SockRef, TcpKeepalive};

// Reach the underlying TcpStream through tokio's MaybeTlsStream → TcpStream
// chain. tokio-tungstenite 0.29 exposes the inner stream via
// `WebSocketStream::get_ref()`. For plain ws:// (our LA endpoint, per
// docs/SECURITY.md), `MaybeTlsStream::Plain(TcpStream)` is the inner.
let tcp_stream: &tokio::net::TcpStream = match ws.get_ref() {
    tokio_tungstenite::MaybeTlsStream::Plain(s) => s,
    _ => anyhow::bail!("LA WS unexpectedly wrapped in TLS; design is ws:// per docs/SECURITY.md"),
};
let sock_ref = SockRef::from(tcp_stream);

// Idle 10 s, probe every 5 s, fail after 3 unanswered probes.
// Worst-case TCP-dead detection: 10 + 3*5 = 25 s. No LA-perf coupling.
let keepalive = TcpKeepalive::new()
    .with_time(Duration::from_secs(10))
    .with_interval(Duration::from_secs(5))
    .with_retries(3);
sock_ref.set_tcp_keepalive(&keepalive)
    .context("set_tcp_keepalive on LA WS socket")?;

// Bound transmit-stall on the send side: if the kernel cannot get any TCP
// ACK for an in-flight segment within 25 s, the next write/read on this
// socket returns ETIMEDOUT. Linux-specific (TCP_USER_TIMEOUT, RFC 5482).
sock_ref.set_tcp_user_timeout(Some(Duration::from_secs(25)))
    .context("set_tcp_user_timeout on LA WS socket")?;
19.4.1.2 Defense layer 2 — application-level reply deadline

Catches app-hung-but-TCP-alive. This necessarily couples to a wall-clock bound on LA's per-frame latency, because TCP cannot see application hangs. We pick the bound generously so the architecture remains agnostic to specific model perf:

use std::time::Duration;
use tokio::time::timeout;

/// Maximum wall-clock time the demo server waits for ANY single
/// LocateAnything-3B reply after sending a frame.
///
/// Chosen generously to cover any plausible model — LocateAnything-3B's
/// measured p95 slow-mode inference is ~1.61 s per
/// docs/DRONE_DETECTION.md:117; a future variant could be 5× slower
/// without changing the protocol. Setting this to 30 s gives ~18× headroom
/// over the worst measured tail, while still bounding silent-stall
/// detection to half a minute. If a future model legitimately needs > 30 s
/// per frame for our use case, the operator bumps this single constant.
///
/// NOTE on the LA-side timer: per docs/CLIENT_PROTOCOL.md the LA worker
/// has its own configurable inference timeout (default ~600 s) above which
/// it returns type:"error" rather than hanging. Our 30 s budget here is a
/// belt against the case where even that timeout fails to fire (worker
/// crashed mid-inference, Python GIL deadlock, CUDA driver hang).
const LA_REPLY_TIMEOUT: Duration = Duration::from_secs(30);

let reply = timeout(LA_REPLY_TIMEOUT, ws.next())
    .await
    .map_err(|_elapsed| anyhow::anyhow!(
        "LA WS reply timeout: no reply received within {:?} after sending frame_id={} \
         (LA process likely hung — TCP keepalive did not trigger because LA's kernel \
         is still ACKing probes; see Section 19.4.1.2)",
        LA_REPLY_TIMEOUT, frame_id
    ))?
    .ok_or_else(|| anyhow::anyhow!("LA WS stream ended mid-frame for frame_id={frame_id}"))??;
19.4.1.3 Properties of the combined two-layer design
  • Coverage is complete: Layer 1 catches every TCP-dead variant within ~25 s. Layer 2 catches every app-hung variant within 30 s. There is no failure mode of an unresponsive LA that escapes both.
  • Loud failure: on either trigger, the LA WS client task returns an anyhow::Error with the full chained context (including which layer fired) → propagates to FsmCommand::LaDown { error } → FSM transitions Streaming/Claimed to Draining with code "la_unreachable" exactly as Section 19.4 step 1 specifies. No silent fallback.
  • Sovereign: uses standard Linux kernel sockopts via the socket2 crate (pinned at =0.6.4; see Section 7.4) plus tokio::time::timeout, both already in the dependency closure.
  • Layer 2's 30 s constant is the only application-level wall-clock number in the LA pipeline. The cadence-driving loop (Section 19.1) remains event-driven (watch::changed); the 30 s budget is a circuit-breaker, not a scheduler. Acceptable per Section 3 because the alternative (no timeout at all) leaves an open silent-stall hole; TCP cannot see app hangs.

Behavior on disconnect (strict, no advisory sub-state): the WebAssembly streamer sees a single user-facing error and the Finite State Machine releases the streamer slot:

  1. On WebSocket close or send/receive error, the LocateAnything-3B client task emits FsmCommand::LaDown { error: anyhow::Error } into the Finite State Machine command channel exactly once per disconnect, then enters the back-off retry loop.
  2. The Finite State Machine, on receiving LaDown while in Streaming or Claimed: transitions to Draining with reason "la_unreachable: <chained-anyhow-string>", which propagates to the streamer as SignalMessage::Error { code: "la_unreachable", message: ... } and to every viewer as a DataChannel "bye" with the same message. The streamer's WebAssembly user interface displays the chained error string and reverts to the prompt-builder grid.
  3. The LocateAnything-3B client task continues retrying in the background. When a reconnect succeeds, it emits FsmCommand::LaUp, which the Finite State Machine logs at info! level. The next user to visit the demo can claim the streamer slot from Idle; the previous streamer must re-claim.
  4. While the LocateAnything-3B server is unreachable, the Finite State Machine refuses new Claim attempts with SignalMessage::Error { code: "la_unreachable", message: ... }. The error message is the most recent chained-anyhow string from the LocateAnything-3B client task. Refusal is loud, not silent.
  5. The /v1/health Hypertext Transfer Protocol response reports {"status":"degraded","cert_valid":<bool>,"la_reachable":false} while in this state — only boolean bits, never the chained-anyhow error string (those go to the authenticated /v1/info per Section 17.5). Operators monitoring /v1/health see the failure immediately; operators authenticated to /v1/info get the underlying error chain.

Why no "advisory sub-state" and why no silent retry while keeping ownership: the user's principle is "no silent fallback, fail loud, no graceful degradation". A streamer whose bounding-box overlay silently disappears for 30 seconds while the LocateAnything-3B server reboots is exactly the graceful-degradation anti-pattern. Loud failure with explicit ownership release is the correct behavior.

This makes the Finite State Machine a strict four-state machine — Idle, Claimed, Streaming, Draining — with an orthogonal "LocateAnything reachable" bit that is on or off, not a partial sub-state. The "off" bit only ever triggers the explicit transitions described above (Streaming/Claimed → Draining via LaDown; refuse-on-Claim while in Idle with the bit off); it never colors any other state.

20. WebRTC Configuration #

20.1 Codec: H.264 Constrained Baseline Level 3.1 (profile-level-id 42e01f) — FORCED, not negotiated

The target codec is H.264 Constrained Baseline Level 3.1, profile-level-id 42e01f, packetization-mode 1. Level 3.1's nominal cap is 1280-by-720 pixels at 30 frames per second.

Critical correction (verified by source-level research on 2026-06-01):
  1. Google Chrome's libwebrtc internal_encoder_factory.cc:34-41 defines the encoder factory order as VP8, H.264, AV1, VP9. The default SDP offer's m=video codec list therefore has VP8 first, H.264 second. Mozilla Firefox is the same (gecko-dev PeerConnectionImpl.cpp:2056-2087: VP8, VP9, H.264 variants, AV1). Only Apple Safari (WebKit's RTCDefaultVideoEncoderFactory.m:54-105) emits H.264 first — and Safari's preferred H.264 entry is profile-level-id 640c1f (Constrained High), not 42e01f.
  2. str0m 0.20.0's SDP answer (verified at src/change/sdp.rs:1483-1498 and src/format/codec_config.rs:65-76) iterates the local codec_config.params insertion order, NOT the offerer's order, filtered against the offer's payload-types. Default codec_config enables VP8 BEFORE H.264. Therefore a default str0m configuration answers any Chrome offer with VP8-first; per RFC 3264 §6.1, Chrome then sends VP8. This breaks our H.264-only decode pipeline.
The defense is belt-and-braces: configure both ends to advertise H.264 only.

20.1.1 Server-side: force str0m to advertise H.264 only

In the str0m::Rtc::builder() chain (Section 20.4), call .clear_codecs() followed by .enable_h264(true). Do NOT call .enable_vp8(...), .enable_vp9(...), or .enable_av1(...). After clear_codecs, str0m's codec_config.params is empty; enable_h264(true) inserts seven H.264 payload-type entries (verified against src/format/codec_config.rs:243-261 on 2026-06-02): profiles 42001f, 42e01f, and 4d001f each in BOTH packetization-mode 1 and packetization-mode 0, PLUS 64001f in packetization-mode 1 ONLY — that is 3×2 + 1 = 7, not 8, because 64001f has no packetization-mode-0 variant (this is why the count is seven). The 42e01f packetization-mode-1 entry (payload type 108) is the one our pipeline expects. The resulting answer m-line lists only H.264 payload-types that intersect the offer, and any offer that doesn't carry H.264 results in a clean SDP-negotiation failure.

20.1.2 Client-side: WebAssembly calls setCodecPreferences() BEFORE createOffer()

To prevent Apple Safari from preferring 640c1f (Constrained High) over the demo's required 42e01f (Constrained Baseline), and to prevent any browser from offering VP8/VP9/AV1 at all (defense-in-depth on top of 20.1.1), the WebAssembly client calls RtcRtpTransceiver::set_codec_preferences() on the video transceiver before pc.createOffer(). The preference list contains only H.264 RtcRtpCodecCapability entries whose sdp_fmtp_line contains both profile-level-id=42e01f AND packetization-mode=1, plus pass-through entries for video/rtx, video/red, and video/ulpfec (required by Chromium per pc/rtp_transceiver.cc:456 and media/session/media_session.cc:633-2237).

Reference code shape in the WebAssembly client (wasm-client/src/rtc.rs):

use anyhow::{Context, Result};
use js_sys::Array;
use wasm_bindgen::{JsCast, JsValue};
use web_sys::{RtcRtpCodecCapability, RtcRtpSender, RtcRtpTransceiver};

pub fn force_h264_baseline_only(transceiver: &RtcRtpTransceiver) -> Result<()> {
    let caps = RtcRtpSender::get_capabilities("video")
        .ok_or_else(|| anyhow::anyhow!("RtcRtpSender::get_capabilities returned None — \
                                       not a secure context, or WebRTC unsupported"))?;
    // `caps.codecs()` in web-sys 0.3.99 is a DEPRECATED setter that returns
    // `&mut Self`; the getter is `get_codecs(&self) -> Array` (verified at
    // https://docs.rs/web-sys/0.3.99/src/web_sys/features/gen_RtcRtpCapabilities.rs.html#17).
    // The Array is never null per the WebIDL contract; emptiness is handled
    // by the `kept.length() == 0` guard at the end of this function.
    let codecs_array: Array = caps.get_codecs();
    let kept = Array::new();
    for cap_jsvalue in codecs_array.iter() {
        let codec: RtcRtpCodecCapability = cap_jsvalue.unchecked_into();
        let mime = codec.get_mime_type();
        let is_h264 = mime.eq_ignore_ascii_case("video/H264");
        let is_passthrough = mime.eq_ignore_ascii_case("video/rtx")
            || mime.eq_ignore_ascii_case("video/red")
            || mime.eq_ignore_ascii_case("video/ulpfec");
        if is_passthrough {
            kept.push(&JsValue::from(codec));
            continue;
        }
        if !is_h264 {
            continue;
        }
        // STRICT: a codec capability without sdp_fmtp_line is unmatched by
        // definition; skip explicitly rather than coercing absent to empty
        // string and falling through the predicate (which would happen to
        // produce the right outcome but with sloppy semantics).
        let Some(fmtp) = codec.get_sdp_fmtp_line() else { continue; };
        if fmtp.contains("profile-level-id=42e01f")
            && fmtp.contains("packetization-mode=1")
        {
            kept.push(&JsValue::from(codec));
        }
    }
    if kept.length() == 0 {
        anyhow::bail!("no acceptable H.264 codec capability (profile-level-id=42e01f, \
                       packetization-mode=1) available from this browser");
    }
    // CORRECTION (verified at web-sys-0.3.99/src/features/gen_RtcRtpTransceiver.rs:95):
    // set_codec_preferences takes &JsValue (a single JS value representing an
    // array of RtcRtpCodec entries), NOT &[JsValue]. We pass the js_sys::Array
    // we built directly via its AsRef<JsValue> impl.
    transceiver.set_codec_preferences(kept.as_ref());
    Ok(())
}
Call sites: after adding the video track via pc.add_track_0(&video_track, &media_stream) (verified at web-sys 0.3.99 src/features/gen_RtcPeerConnection.rs:538-542; the _0 suffix counts EXTRA streams, not total args — the track + first stream are still required positional args), or via the alternative 3-arg pc.add_track(&video_track, &media_stream, &js_sys::Array::new()) form, retrieve the resulting transceiver via pc.get_transceivers(), find the video one, call force_h264_baseline_only(&transceiver). If this returns Err, render the chained error string to the user via the last_error reactive signal and abort the streaming attempt. The user sees "no acceptable H.264 codec available in this browser" and knows their browser cannot stream to the demo.

Notes verified against web-sys 0.3.99:

  • RtcRtpSender::get_capabilities("video") is a STATIC call on the type, not an instance method (the binding declares static_method_of = "RtcRtpSender" at gen_RtcRtpSender.rs:103). Requires features RtcRtpSender AND RtcRtpCapabilities both enabled.
  • set_codec_preferences takes &JsValue, not &[JsValue] (verified at gen_RtcRtpTransceiver.rs:95). We pass the js_sys::Array as kept.as_ref() — js_sys::Array implements AsRef<JsValue>.
  • Implementation must enable features RtcRtpTransceiver, RtcRtpCodecCapability, RtcRtpCapabilities in web-sys's feature list (per Section 7.5).

20.1.3 Failure mode if H.264 is unavailable on a specific browser

On a browser without H.264 capability (extremely rare in 2026 — only some highly-restricted Chromium builds with H.264 compiled out), the client-side call returns the "no acceptable H.264 codec capability" error. The user-visible message identifies the cause. The server side's clear_codecs + enable_h264 means: even if a misbehaving client offers VP8, the server's answer either matches H.264 (success) or produces an empty intersection causing the SDP exchange to fail with a clean error.

20.2 Capture parameters (browser-side getUserMedia constraints)

{
    video: {
        width:      { ideal: 1280, max: 1280 },   // max == ideal: pinned to the Level 3.1 ceiling (§3.4, §20.1)
        height:     { ideal: 720,  max: 720  },    // 1280x720 = 3600 macroblocks = Level 3.1 MaxFS; 1080p is unencodable at 42e01f
        frameRate:  { ideal: 30,   max: 30   },
        facingMode: { ideal: "environment" },     // prefer rear camera; user can override via deviceId
    },
    audio: false,
}

Use ideal, never exact — this avoids OverconstrainedError on devices that cannot satisfy exact constraints. The max is pinned to 1280×720, identical to the ideal: that is the H.264 Constrained Baseline Level 3.1 frame-size ceiling (3600 macroblocks; §3.4, §20.1), and any capture with width ≤ 1280 AND height ≤ 720 is ≤ 3600 macroblocks, so it is always encodable in-level. A higher max would let a camera deliver frames the forced 42e01f encoder cannot represent in-level — and libwebrtc does not downscale to the negotiated level (it leaves OpenH264 at LEVEL_UNKNOWN, which derives the level from the actual frame size, emitting an out-of-level bitstream), so capture time is the only safe place to enforce the ceiling. Because the constraint is max (not exact/min), a camera whose native modes exceed 720p is downsampled to satisfy it rather than rejected (every current browser does this; Firefox gained arbitrary crop-and-scale in v144).

Per the W3C Media Capture and Streams specification's Fitness Distance and SelectSettings algorithms (https://www.w3.org/TR/mediacapture-streams/#dfn-fitness-distance), browsers behave as follows: max is a required upper bound (the device's settings dictionary is rejected if it exceeds the max); ideal contributes a finite fitness distance based on |actual - ideal| / max(|actual|, |ideal|); the device's actual capabilities are queried, the closest match is returned. Cameras that cannot deliver 720p (older webcams maxing at 480p, ultra-low-end embedded cameras at 320p) return that lower resolution rather than triggering OverconstrainedError. The browser cannot upsample beyond native; it can downsample.

Therefore the WebAssembly client must validate the actual capture dimensions after getUserMedia resolves, before adding the track to the WebRTC RTCPeerConnection:

// wasm-client/src/camera.rs
let track: web_sys::MediaStreamTrack = stream
    .get_video_tracks()
    .get(0)
    .dyn_into()
    .map_err(js_err("MediaStream has no video track"))?;
let settings = track.get_settings();
let width  = settings.get_width().ok_or_else(|| anyhow!(
    "MediaTrackSettings.width missing after getUserMedia"))?;
let height = settings.get_height().ok_or_else(|| anyhow!(
    "MediaTrackSettings.height missing after getUserMedia"))?;

// Single source of truth for the capture-dimension policy — the demo floor AND
// the H.264 Level 3.1 ceiling — called here AND on the mid-call camera switch
// (§21.6), so the two paths can never drift. On refusal, stop the track so the
// camera light goes off; on a sub-ideal-but-acceptable capture, surface the warning.
match check_capture_dimensions(width, height) {
    Err(e)        => { track.stop(); return Err(e); }
    Ok(Some(msg)) => last_warning.set(Some(msg)),
    Ok(None)      => {}
}

// Defined once at module scope; shared by both capture paths (initial + switch).
fn check_capture_dimensions(width: i32, height: i32) -> anyhow::Result<Option<String>> {
    const HARD_FAIL_WIDTH:  i32 = 640;   // sub-VGA: obsolete; refuse to stream
    const HARD_FAIL_HEIGHT: i32 = 360;   // 16:9 floor matching 640 width
    const WARN_WIDTH:       i32 = 1280;  // below 720p: degraded LA token density
    const WARN_HEIGHT:      i32 = 720;
    const CEILING_WIDTH:    i32 = 1280;  // ABOVE this = out-of-level under forced 42e01f
    const CEILING_HEIGHT:   i32 = 720;   // 1280x720 = 3600 MBs = Level 3.1 MaxFS (§3.4, §20.1)

    if width < HARD_FAIL_WIDTH || height < HARD_FAIL_HEIGHT {
        anyhow::bail!(
            "Camera resolution {width}x{height} is below the required minimum \
             of {HARD_FAIL_WIDTH}x{HARD_FAIL_HEIGHT}. This demo requires at \
             least Video Graphics Array resolution to produce reliable \
             bounding-box detections from LocateAnything-3B."
        );
    }
    if width > CEILING_WIDTH || height > CEILING_HEIGHT {
        // Defense-in-depth: the getUserMedia `max` already pins capture at the Level
        // 3.1 ceiling, so this guards only against a user agent that ignores `max`. A
        // frame wider than 1280 OR taller than 720 under the forced 42e01f would put
        // an OUT-OF-LEVEL H.264 bitstream on the wire (libwebrtc does NOT downscale to
        // the negotiated level), which the SFU and viewers would silently decode —
        // exactly the silent anomaly Section 3 forbids. Fail loud instead.
        anyhow::bail!(
            "Camera delivered {width}x{height}, above the H.264 Constrained Baseline \
             Level 3.1 ceiling of {CEILING_WIDTH}x{CEILING_HEIGHT} that the capture \
             constraints pin. Refusing to encode an out-of-level bitstream."
        );
    }
    if width < WARN_WIDTH || height < WARN_HEIGHT {
        return Ok(Some(format!(
            "Note: camera is providing {width}x{height}. The demo prefers \
             {WARN_WIDTH}x{WARN_HEIGHT} for best results. Detections may be \
             less reliable on smaller objects."
        )));
    }
    Ok(None)
}

The hard-fail threshold of 640x360 is the Video Graphics Array floor — the conventional minimum for modern web video. Anything below it almost certainly indicates an obsolete or misconfigured device that won't produce useful object-detection inputs.

Source verification: ffmpeg-next 7.1.0's frame::Video::width() / height() (verified at rust-ffmpeg/src/util/frame/video.rs:112-126) read directly from AVFrame's per-frame dimensions, populated by libavcodec when a new Sequence Parameter Set arrives mid-stream. libswscale's Context must be re-created on any dimension change. RFC 6184 §8.4 allows in-band parameter-set updates; the streamer's browser handles this transparently when the camera resolution changes (e.g., during RTCRtpSender.replaceTrack for the mid-call camera switch in Section 21.6).

20.3 SDP answer fragments (server emits these)

  • profile-level-id=42e01f mirrored in the answer's a=fmtp: line.
  • level-asymmetry-allowed=1 in the same line (RFC 6184 §8.2.2; permits send-side level to differ from receive-side).
  • packetization-mode=1 (Single Time Aggregation Packet for fragmented NAL Units; RFC 7742 requires every WebRTC implementation to support mode 1, with mode 0 optional).
  • b=AS:2500 AND b=TIAS:2500000 on the m=video line. Important: str0m 0.20.0 hard-codes bw: None in its generated Session Description Protocol (verified at str0m-src/src/change/sdp.rs:932, 1438, 1558 and the formatter at src/sdp/data.rs:1250-1252, 1320-1322). So this line is NOT emitted automatically. The demo server must post-process the SDP answer string before sending it to the streamer: insert BOTH b=AS:2500\r\n (Application-Specific bandwidth hint, kilobits per second) AND b=TIAS:2500000\r\n (Transport-Independent Application-Specific in bits per second, per RFC 3890) immediately after the matching m=video line.
  • Why both, unconditionally: Mozilla Firefox's WebRTC stack (gecko-dev/dom/media/webrtc/jsep/JsepTrack.cpp:415) reads ONLY b=TIAS; the same file at line 430 has an open TODO ("add support for b=AS if TIAS is not set; bug 976521") that remains unimplemented in 2026. Google Chrome's libwebrtc (pc/webrtc_sdp.cc:3018-3060) parses both modifiers but stores only the last one seen; emitting b=AS:2500 then b=TIAS:2500000 ends with Chrome holding bandwidth=2,500,000 bps and bandwidth_type="TIAS" — semantically identical to b=AS:2500. Apple Safari (WebKit's libwebrtc fork) follows Chrome's parser. Emitting both is harmless on Chrome/Safari and required for Firefox. The plan does NOT inspect User-Agent or otherwise detect browser — both lines are always emitted.
  • Where the post-processing lives: in server/src/sdp_postprocess.rs, exposing one function pub fn inject_bandwidth_caps(sdp: String, kbps: u32) -> anyhow::Result<String>. The WebSocket handler in server/src/signaling.rs calls it between sdp_api().accept_offer(offer)?.to_sdp_string() and the SignalMessage::SdpAnswer JSON write (per Section 11.8 step 6). The function locates every m=video line in the input string and inserts the two b=... lines immediately after it (preserving the existing c= line position; per RFC 4566 §5.8 the bandwidth lines may follow either the session-level or media-level c=, and modern browsers parse both placements). Implementation is a simple line-by-line scan; no regex needed.
  • The b=AS/b=TIAS values cap the encoder's initial bitrate; GoogCC over TWCC takes over after startup and may bring it lower under congestion.

20.4 str0m configuration

let mut rtc = str0m::Rtc::builder()
    .clear_codecs()                                     // drops default VP8/VP9/AV1/H.264/Opus
    .enable_h264(true)                                  // re-enables ONLY H.264 (4 profiles × 2 packetization-modes)
    // intentionally NOT calling enable_vp8 / enable_vp9 / enable_av1
    // intentionally NOT calling enable_opus (we use audio: false on the streamer)
    .enable_bwe(Some(str0m::bwe::Bitrate::kbps(2500))) // seeds BWE
    .set_ice_lite(true)                                 // ICE controlling role pinned to SFU; see note below
    // Add own-host candidates explicitly. NO auto-interface-discovery.
    .build(std::time::Instant::now());                  // str0m 0.20.0 build(Instant) is required

for resolved_addr in resolved_public_host_addrs {  // IPv4-only, A records of --public-host; see tls.rs
    let socket_addr = std::net::SocketAddr::new(resolved_addr, 51000);
    let candidate = str0m::Candidate::host(socket_addr, "udp")
        .context("Candidate::host construction")?;
    rtc.add_local_candidate(candidate);
}

The clear_codecs().enable_h264(true) sequence is load-bearing — it forces str0m's SDP answer to advertise H.264 payload-types only. Without it, the default codec_config has VP8 listed first and Chrome's offer (which also lists VP8 first) gets a VP8-first answer, leading to VP8 negotiation. The H.264 entries inserted by enable_h264(true) include profile-level-id 42e01f packetization-mode 1 — the exact entry our pipeline expects.

What enable_bwe(Some(Bitrate::kbps(2500))) actually does: per str0m-src/src/config.rs:352-369, this is the seed for the send-side Bandwidth Estimator on this Rtc instance. It governs str0m's own pacing decisions when sending RTP packets — relevant for RTX (retransmissions) and forward-keyframe traffic to the streamer, AND for the SFU→viewer pacer. It does NOT cause str0m to advertise a bandwidth in the SDP (see Section 20.3 note about b=AS requiring post-processing). The estimator is fed by bwe.update(records, now) at src/session.rs:618-620 based on incoming TWCC feedback about packets str0m sent. The estimate surfaces as Event::EgressBitrateEstimate(BweKind::Twcc(bitrate)) (src/lib.rs:971). For our SFU, this means each viewer's Rtc instance produces its own egress bitrate estimate; that estimate is surfaced for observability only — the SFU does NOT act on it to police, throttle, or evict a viewer (fire-and-forget; see Section 20.10, which records why per-viewer eviction was considered and rejected).

20.5 Browser ICE configuration (server sends to browser via SignalMessage)

{
    "iceServers": [
        { "urls": [ "stun:<public-host>:51000" ] }
    ]
}

The <public-host> is our own server, our own STUN. No third-party STUN. No TURN configured (deferred per Section 26).

On the WebAssembly client side, construct the RtcConfiguration by calling RtcConfiguration::new() then the modern setter RtcConfiguration::set_ice_servers(&JsValue) (verified at web-sys-0.3.99/src/features/gen_RtcConfiguration.rs:44). Do NOT use the older builder-style ice_servers(&mut self, &JsValue) at line 89; it is marked deprecated in web-sys 0.3.99. Pattern:

use wasm_bindgen::JsValue;
use web_sys::{RtcConfiguration, RtcIceServer};

let stun_server = RtcIceServer::new();
let urls = js_sys::Array::new();
urls.push(&JsValue::from_str("stun:demo.example.com:51000"));  // or the actual public-host
stun_server.set_urls(&urls);

let ice_servers_array = js_sys::Array::new();
ice_servers_array.push(&stun_server);

let cfg = RtcConfiguration::new();
cfg.set_ice_servers(&ice_servers_array);

let pc = web_sys::RtcPeerConnection::new_with_configuration(&cfg)
    .map_err(js_err("RtcPeerConnection::new_with_configuration"))?;

20.6 Keyframe handling — event-driven only, no scheduler

  • Use KeyframeRequestKind::Pli (Picture Loss Indication), NOT Fir (Full Intra Request). FIR is reserved for mixer/composition scenarios per RFC 5104 and is explicitly disallowed for loss recovery. The str0m example file examples/chat.rs:444 uses Fir; this is wrong per the RFC; the implementation agent MUST use Pli.
  • PLI emitter #1 — new-viewer attach. Proactively send a Picture Loss Indication to the streamer when a new viewer's outbound video is ready, saving approximately one round-trip-time of black frames at the viewer's start. Implementation: when an Event::MediaAdded fires for a viewer Rtc's outbound video Mid, look up the streamer Rtc, find the inbound video stream, call streamer_rtc.writer(STREAMER_VIDEO_MID).request_keyframe(None, KeyframeRequestKind::Pli).
  • PLI emitter #2 — decoder-loss recovery. The decode-pipeline thread (Section 19.1) observes MediaData.contiguous == false when str0m's depacketizer has exhausted its hold-back window without recovering a lost packet via NACK/RTX. The thread calls decoder.flush() AND signals the FSM via FsmCommand::RequestStreamerPli (blocking_send) to emit one KeyframeRequestKind::Pli upstream to the streamer. This is the str0m reference pattern (examples/chat.rs:442-445) verbatim — one event-driven PLI per packet-loss recovery event.
  • No periodic scheduler, no application-level throttle. Two structural rate caps already sit below the SFU's application code: (i) str0m's pending_request_keyframe: Option<KeyframeRequestKind> field at src/streams/receive.rs:80 is single-slot — request_keyframe() assigns, so multiple calls within one drain window collapse; the StreamRx::pending_request_keyframe field is drained inside Session::handle_timeout → Streams::handle_timeout → StreamRx::maybe_create_keyframe_request (src/session.rs:234, src/streams/mod.rs:429, src/streams/receive.rs:506) and the dominant recurring wakeup that pumps it is the 50-millisecond TWCC_INTERVAL (src/session.rs:42), so the wall-clock cap on outbound PLI is ≤ one per ~50 ms. (This is the inbound StreamRx path — the SFU asking the streamer for an IDR. Do not confuse it with the separate StreamTx::pending_request_keyframe at src/streams/send.rs:114, drained via Session::poll_event → Streams::poll_keyframe_request, which is the forward-to-viewer direction and is not used here.) (ii) libwebrtc's kMinKeyframeSendIntervalMs = 300 at video/encoder_rtcp_feedback.cc:23-24 drops any PLI received within 300 ms of the last emitted IDR. The streamer's encoder physically cannot produce IDRs faster than ~3.3/second regardless of how many PLIs we issue. An application-level throttle on top of these two structural floors would be a third redundant layer with no behavioral effect; omitting it honors the no-overengineered-options principle of Section 3.
  • No periodic IDR cadence imposed by the demo server. The browser's encoder emits IDRs on its own natural cadence (libwebrtc default keyFrameInterval = 3000 frames per api/video_codecs/video_encoder.cc:36, ≈ 100 seconds at 30 fps), plus on the two event-driven triggers above. Sampling to LocateAnything-3B is driven by LA's own per-frame latency via the watch-channel pattern (Section 19.1); the demo server runs no timer-driven PLI loop.

20.7 NACK and RTX

str0m's StreamTx maintains a per-egress RTX (retransmission) cache of 1000 packets × 3 seconds default duration for video. Correction (verified against str0m 0.20.0 source on 2026-06-02): the RtxCache::new(2000, …) at src/streams/send.rs:275 is a transient constructor default that StreamTx::set_rtx_cache immediately overwrites with RtcConfig::send_buffer_video — whose default is 1000 (src/config.rs:641) — on BOTH the Session-Description-Protocol negotiation path (src/change/sdp.rs:1025-1034) and the direct-API path (src/change/direct.rs:314); the cache duration is DEFAULT_RTX_CACHE_DURATION = 3 s (src/streams/send.rs:38). So the live per-viewer RTX cache is 1000 packets, not the 2000 earlier drafts claimed. Viewer-side packet loss triggers Negative Acknowledgements that the SFU answers from its own local cache — the SFU does NOT NACK upstream to the streamer on the viewer's behalf. This is the correct SFU pattern.

20.8 Transport Wide Congestion Control — what it actually covers

str0m 0.20.0 advertises the transport-cc RTP header extension by default (verified at str0m-src/src/rtp/ext.rs:196-198, 375 and src/sdp/parser.rs:1133, 1141). The SFU's receive register records the arrival times of every RTP packet (src/session.rs:519-523) and emits RTCP transport-cc Feedback Message reports every 50 milliseconds (src/session.rs:42, 249-253, 301-313). The streamer's browser receives those reports and runs its own GoogCC congestion-controller, which calls VideoStreamEncoder::OnBitrateUpdated and ultimately the H.264 encoder's SetRates method.
